â€˜à¤°à¤¦à¤¤à¥à¤ªà¤¥à¤ƒ’ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¸à¤ªà¥à¤¤à¤°à¥à¤šà¤‚ à¤¸à¤ªà¥à¤¤à¤¦à¤¶à¤‚ à¤¸à¥‚à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤‚ à¤µà¤¸à¤¿à¤·à¥à¤ à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤°à¥à¤·à¤‚ à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥ˆà¤·à¥à¤Ÿà¥à¤­à¤‚ à¤µà¤¾à¤°à¥à¤£à¤®à¥ à¥¤ à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤•à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤‚ à¤š â€“ à¤°à¤¦à¤¤à¥à¤¸à¤ªà¥à¤¤ ’ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¥¤ à¤—à¤¤à¥‹ à¤µà¤¿à¤¨à¤¿à¤¯à¥‹à¤—à¤ƒ à¥¥

Jamison Brereton

87 (603)
VarunÌ£a
Vasiá¹£á¹­ha MaitrÄvaruá¹‡i
7 verses: triá¹£á¹­ubh
On this hymn, see the introduction to the previous hymn and especially Jamison (2007: 101â€“3).
The name of Varuá¹‡a occurs in each verse of the hymn, and in verse 4 Varuá¹‡a himself speaks. Like the previous one, therefore, this hymn has an omphalos structure, organized around the central verse 4 and the words of the god himself. In hymns organized around a central verse, that verse is often the key to under
standing a hymn or the dramatic climax of the hymn, as it is, for example, in the previous hymn. But sometimes, as here, it is the most enigmatic verse of all. In 4a, as implied in the translation below, does Varuá¹‡a know that the poet is wise and therefore will understand his cryptic words? Or does meÌdhira â€œwiseâ€ anticipate the wisdom that the poet will receive from Varuá¹‡aâ€™s words? In that case, we might rather say that Varuá¹‡a spoke â€œto me (to make me) wiseâ€? As Jamison (2007: 102â€“ 3) argues, the ambiguous placement of naÌ in 4c allows it to be taken either as a particle of comparison (â€œlikeâ€) or as a negation (â€œnotâ€). As a result the pÄda can mean either â€œhe will speak (their names) like secrets,â€ that is, he will speak the names but he will do so only in a cryptic manner, or â€œhe will not speak their secret (names),â€ that is, he will keep the secrets hidden. But most of all, there are the obscure words of the god in 4b: â€œThe inviolable cow bears three times seven names.â€ As often, the â€œcowâ€ in this verse is speech (e.g., Thompson 1995: 20), and it is speech, or more specifically this hymn, that carries within it twenty-one â€œnames.â€ These names may be similar to the three times seven â€œtracksâ€ (padÄÌ) in I.72.6, although these tracks too are also â€œsecretâ€ (gÃºhyÄni) and therefore their reference unclear.
There is one possible clue to the twenty-one names in the following verse. Verse 5 mentions three heavens and three earths â€œarranged in sixâ€ or â€œarranged in sixes.â€ That is, â€œsixâ€ could refer to the three heavens and three earths, and, if so, we should translate, â€œThree heavens are hidden within him, and below are three earths, form
ing an arrangement of six.â€ On the other hand, if á¹£aÌá¸vidhÄnÄá¸¥ refers only to the three earths and if each of the three earths is arranged in six, then we would have eighteen earths and three heavens, which gives twenty-one, the number of â€œnames.â€ In that case, the â€œnamesâ€ might be tokens of the extent of Varuá¹‡aâ€™s rule.
Returning again to verse 4, its last mystery is the â€œlater generationâ€ in 4d, which may refer not to a future generation of human beings but to humans as the â€œgenerationâ€ of beings later than the gods. If so, then Varuá¹‡a strives to instruct humans despite their limited capacities, perhaps well illustrated in our attempts to understand him.
While verse 4 is the most enigmatic in the hymn, it is not the only mysterious one. We have already touched on the riddle of three heavens and three earths of verse 5ab. In verse 6a Varuá¹‡a is like the sky, which drops to the horizon at the far distance where there is the river that surrounds the earth. Because he is like the sky that drops toward this river, he is called a wild animal (6b) that comes down to the water to drink and he is like the â€œbright drop,â€ which, as Geldner points out, might be the setting moon or sun or even the soma as it sinks into the waters with which it is mixed. In 6c Varuá¹‡a is â€œof deep recitation.â€ The primary sense is probably that Varuá¹‡a speaks profound words, but the compound is ambiguous and could also mean that Varuá¹‡a inspires or receives profound praise-recitations. Verse 7 connects this hymn to the previous hymn and provides a persuasive reason to link the two hymns, as Jamison (2007: 103) has noted. In VII.86.2 the poet asks, â€œWhen shall I be within Varuá¹‡a?â€ Here in verse 7 the poet gives an answer: when we are free of offense, then â€œwe would be within Varuá¹‡a.â€ That is, we would be in conformity with the vratÄÌny aÌditeá¸¥ â€œthe commandments of Aditi,â€ the goddess representing offense lessness, whose commandments are mentioned in 7c.

â€œVaruá¹‡a prepared a path for the sun; he set free the waters of the rivers genitive rated in the firmament;hastening (to his task), as a horse let loose rushes to (a flock of) mares, he divided the great nights from thedays.â€

Jamison Brereton

Varuá¹‡a dug the paths for the sun. Forward (went) the floods of rivers to the sea,
(those) mares, like a surge sent surging, following the truth. He made great streambeds for the days.

Griffith

VARUNA cut a pathway out for Surya, and led the watery floods of rivers onward.
The Mares, as in a race, speed on in order. He made great channels for the days to follow.

Jamison Brereton

Your breath, the wind, roars again and again through airy space, like an ardent animal [=stallion], victorious in its pasture.
(Here are) all your own domains, Varuá¹‡a, between these two great and lofty world-halves.994 VII.88

Griffith

The wind, thy breath, hath sounded through the region like a wild beast that seeks his food in pastures.
Within these two, exalted Earth and Heaven, O Varuna, are all the forms thou lovest.

Geldner

Dein Odem schnaubt als Wind in den Luftraum wie ein ungeduldiges Tier, das auf der Weide den Kampf gewonnen hat. Zwischen diesen beiden groÃŸen, hohen Welten sind alles deine lieben Erscheinungsformen, o Varuna.

Dein Hauch, der Wind, durchrauscht den weiten Luftraum, wie gier’ge Heerden, die im Grase schwelgen; Und zwischen diesen beiden weiten Welten sind, Varuna, dir lieb die StÃ¤tten alle.
